A HOPELESS CASE.
THE report that a number of the so-called
"county"' officials elected in this city at the last
election contemplate inaugurating litigation by
which the question whether they have been chosen
under the county government act or under the new
charter may be decided by the Supreme Court brings
up a political subject of considerable interest. Of
course all these officials were elected with the under
standing that they would serve one year, but, not
withstanding that fact, it is not difficult to sympa
thize with them in their effort to hold on for four
years. An office is a precious thing, and to give it
up is always heart-rending.
But physical pain should not induce anybody to
undertake so hopeless a lawsuit as the one the local
officials in question have under consideration. There .
is no doubt whatever that the Freeholders who framed '
the new charter possessed the power to fix the terms ;
of all municipal officers and employes. In 1896 the
people adopted a constitutional amendment declaring
among other things that it shall be competent in all
charters framed under section 8 of article XI of the
lie bw "to provide for the manner in which, the
times at which and the terms for which the sev
eral county officers shall be elected or appointed, for
their compensation and for the number of deputies
tnat each shall have, and for the compensation pay
able to each of such deputies."
As if to dispense with all doubt as to the applica
tion of this provision, the same amendment speaks in
terms of city and county governments which shall
have been "merged and consolidated into one muni
cipal government." The only question remaining in
connection with the terms of officials under the new
charter is whether the instrument went into effect in
time to cover the election of last year, in other words,
whether the "county" officials elected in 1898 were
chosen under it or under the State law. But this can
not, it seems to us, enter into the controversy. The
grant of power quoted above in any event would
authorize the charter-makers to shorten the terms of
municipal officials, and it could not make any differ
ence to such officials when the new instruments went
into effect.
Under the circumstances it would appear that the
less time our local government expends in endeavor
ing to hold on the better. The Supreme Court can
not avoid deciding against the so-called "county"
officials. "What these gentlemen should do is to make
a good record and get ready for the fall campaign.
BRUTES ON THE BICYCLE.
SUNDAY a lady riding in the park was fun down
by a scorcher. The fellow was absolutely with
out excuse. He was coasting along the path
â¢with his feet over the handle-bars, a position from
which he could not possibly recover quickly to meet
the emergency likely to arise. Anybody but a fool
would have realized this, and a fool has no business
en a wheel.
The regulations of the park do not permit coasting.
A person guilty of such an offense against common
sense is subject to arrest, and he ought to be subject
to incarceration in an asylum for lunatics. Neverthe
less, the person guilty of subjecting the injured lady
to the peril of death was coasting merrily down
Strawberry Hill, and that his foolhardiness did not re
sult in murder is no credit to himself. Had he killed
the woman he would have gone on, as he did go,
anxious to escape the result of his criminal careless
ness. It is to be hoped that the coward will yet be
caught and made to pay some sort of penalty for his
bnital stupidity.
Thousands of San Francisco people ride the wheel.
They are entitled to protection. Most of them choose
tc ride in the park, because there there is a path for
them, smooth track, and comparatively level grades.
But if a woman-killing nincompoop is not subject to
restraint it appears that they are not sufficiently pro
tected. If every dolt whose brain swells the calf of
his legs is at liberty to maim or slay others who may
desire to ride, the bicycle path loses a measure of its
charm. We suggest that two park policemen be de
tailed to mount bicycles instead of steeds and patrol
the path given to the use of bicyclists. When they
observe an idiot coasting, to the peril of everybody
else, it will be their duty to gather him in and put
him where he will be incapable of harm.
"The American Boy" does not in our judgment
constitute a suitable name for a warship. There are
several ships in prospect, and there is not yet a Cali
fornia.
If Dreyfus would consent to die, and thereby live
up to current rumor, France could afford to extend
him a vote of thanks.
THE CAUCUS TRICK-
TWENTY-SEVEN legislative supporters oi
Daniel M. Burns, who have misrepresented their
constituencies and lowered themselves and the
State by their determination to force his election,
even though it should wreck the Republican party,
have declared their intention to continue the dead
lock at Sacramento unless their kind -of a caucus is
held. These gentlemen were elected from Assembly
! and Senatorial districts, not one of which tolerates
their choice, and every vote they have cast was a
breach of their representative obligations. They in
structed Senator Perkins to vote for the treaty" of
Paris, and against his personal convictions he obeyed
the instruction. But they have arrogantly and con
tumaciously asserted the right to disregard the
voices of the communities from which they came, to
tear instructions to pieces and to gratify their pri
vate inclinations, in whatever manner controlled, in
direct opposition to public sentiment.
The word "caucus" is generally used as a desig
nation of political gatherings to discuss party
measures. It is now proposed to twist its sig
r.ification into a method of securing a unanimous
and secret indorsement of an incompetent and dis
graced candidate for Senatorial honors in such a man
ner that the responsibility shall fall upon an en
tire party, and noi on individual legislators. It is
well that Republicans throughout the State, who are
among its most enterprising, intelligent and hon
ored citizens, should be formally apprised of the
treatment to which it is designed to subject them at
the bidding of the railroad. But they are too inde
pendent and too decent, and Ihey have too much
pride in the history and the achievements of the poli
j tical organization to which they are attached, by the
i tie of principle and not of pelf, to submit to a de
f liberate act of treachery that could not be cor.sum-
I mated in the face of day nor without secret mani
pulation of the most dishonorable nature. No man fit
to be a Senator of the United States could endure the
situation over which Dan Burns fiendishly gloats.
1 With no antecedents to recommend him, calied for by
I no substantial portion of his party, and indeed, out
! side of the Legislature, almost unanimously repu
i dialed because he has the railroad push and the Ex
â¢ aminer behind him, he has braved popular denuncia- :
tion and threatened in terms to deprive the State of j
one representative in the upper house oi. Congress,
and thus to insure the defeat of the party, unless the
prolonged intrigue on his behalf is successful. His
barefaced assumption is that he can turn his minority
into a majority if new recruits can only be saved from !
personal identification.
Oui respect for the Legislature forbids us to anti
cipate anything less than the ignominious failure of
this last and most abominable effort to debase the
Republican party and to place it in the attitude of pro- j
claiming its own shame by accepting the railroad J
falsehoods during the last canvass and the candidacy
it failed to disclose until after the election. These
twenty-seven Senators and Assemblymen, in their
manifesto, which amounts almost to threats and to
menaces within the definitions of the Civil Code, ap
peal to representative institutions as supplying the
logical reason for the conspiracy they label as a de
mand for a caucus. This stuff is unworthy of a de
bate in a corner grocery. When they advocate Burns
whom do they represent? Do they represent the
citizens who elected them? Do they represent their
own families or their neighbors'? Do they repre
sent the intelligence, the educated citizenship, the
manhood, the honor of the State? If they do, the
evidences of these facts, not a scintilla of which has
so far been offered to the public eye or the public
ear, without a caucus, can be disinfected and produced.
Do they represent the railroad? If they do, and that
they do is indisputable, they would not dare to avow
that unspeakable dishonesty as a vindication of the
representative idea or of their fidelity to Republican
principles or to Republican policy. It takes sixty
votes, as the Legislature is now constituted, if all the
members are present, to elect a Senator. If through
a majority of a caucus, a minority of both houses,
thirty-three votes, should be added to the twenty
seven, and by this palpable fraud, against the will of
the Republican party and the people as a whole, Dan
Burns should be chosen, whom would the sixty repre
sent, and at what point would the representative prin
ciple come in? If a majority of a Legislature, which
is the immediate constituency of a Federal Senator,
can disregard their pledges and their constitutional
votes, and, by a secret, unofficial meeting convert that |
minority into a majority, it is too plain for argument
that the principle of representation is trodden into the
dirt and the fundamental theory of American institu
tions contemptuously violated. No chop logic or
brazen declamation can overturn this proposition.
There is not a sentence in the manifesto that is true
or even plausible. Its bold assertions could not de
ceive a child. It falsely parades ignorance, stupidity
and venality as the leaven that raises Republicans
to distinction. It is the mere edict of the railroad,
its last resort when other means have failed to justify
its rampant inebriety by driving its incompetent and
characterless agent like a wedge into the body of the
Senate and by pushing the beaten Democracy far into
the road toward victory.
This spectacular exhibition, -fortunately for the
country, has never before been equaled. Why not
elect Crimmins or Kelly or even Buckley? Any of
them would be as acceptable as Dan Burns to the
citizens of California, who would prefer, however, and
have often demanded, the prompt selection of a Re
publican, a statesman and a gentleman.
& RAID ON THE TREASURY.
THE bill before the Legislature to establish day
schools for deaf mutes has no excuse for being,
except to give somebody or several somebodies
a job at the expense of the taxpayer. It proposes to
have the people pay at the rate of $150 per annum per
capita for the instruction in the public schools of
deaf mutes between the ages of 5 and 21 years, and
adds also the feeble-minded juveniles of imperfect
speech. These two classes are to be brought into the
day schools, to associate with other pupils, and be
taught by the person who is able to round up five of
them and take the $150 apiece which the State is to
pay.
The scheme is insufferable. The State has near
Berkeley an old and perfectly equipped institution for
deaf mutes. They are there taught by the most ad
vanced methods, so that they can enter any walk or
calling that is open to the non-defective classes. Sev
eral have gone from that institution into the State
University, so perfectly trained in lip reading that they
were under no disadvantage in the class and lecture
re oms and laboratory, and graduated just the same as
if they were physically normal. To that institution in
the future as in the past these juvenile deaf mutes
should go to be systematically trained in lip reading
and articulation, in which instruction it has long
ranked foremost in the world.
The utter disregard of the interests of the defective
shown by the day school bill is appalling. Again,
normal children should not be brought during their
infancy in contact with the defective, and especially
THE SAN FBANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MATICH 7, 1899.
not with the feeble-minded. The plan to introduce
the latter into association and daily contact with
young children in the day schools is more idiotic
than any of the proposed beneficiaries of the law.
Wise people have long known that it is not well to
have a nervous, sickly, deformed or physically de
fective teacher for young children, because, through
the influence of suggestion, great harm is wrought
upon the nervous systems of the pupils. This being
so, how much greater harm must come of mixing the
â  feeble-minded, the sad victims of prenatal blight, with
l normal children!
For the feeble-minded there is an institution at
Glen Ellen, where the highest scientific care is de
voted to their development, and they should be sent
there at. as early an age as possible, for if defective
brain cells are to be repaired the process must begin
early.
The bill is a heartless job, devised to advance
some one's selfish interests under a pretense of phil
anthropic regard for the unfortunate. The amount
of money it will take out of the treasury cannot be
fcretold. No oversight is provided for the opera
tions under it, and it is as open to frauds as was the
coyote scalp bounty law. If the Legislature com
mit the folly of passing it, the Governor will find an
opportunity for his veto to serve at Â©nee the unfor
tunate and defend the taxpayers.
THE CLOSING OF THE GflP.
BY the publication of Mr. Huntington's recent
letter to Edward Ivison of Santa Barbara
concerning the delay in closing tlic gap in the
railroad between Elwood and Suri The Call has lU<
cceded in drawing to it the attention Â«â¢! Mrs, Stanford
and Mr. George Crocker, thereby bringing about an
understanding among the dignitaries oi the Southern
Pacific and what promises to be -i speedy undertaking
of the work.
Neither Mrs. Stanford ooi V. i Crocker was
pleased with the insinuation conveyed in the state
ment of the Huntington tetter: "We have succeeded
in getting Mrs. Stanford and George Crocker to
! agree to go on and' close the K.ip." Each denies any
responsibility for the delay. Mr. Crocker states that
Mrs. Stanford and himself, so far from opposing
' railroad construction, have in the past personally
I obligated themselves for large sums of money for such
: work, and Mrs. Stanford, in an interview with the
New York correspondent of The Call, said: "Kindly
say that I am not an obstructionist. On the con
trary. I am in entire sympathy with the railroad in all
! that it proposes to do for the future development of
I California."
When these statements were repeated to Mr. Hun
tington that gentleman suavely replied: "There has
been a disagreement over this matter. We made the
promise years ago to close the gap, and certainly
should have redeemed that promise before now. While
I have always favored the building of this bit of road
ethers have thought it 'best to defer construction."
The statement thus made was qualified by Mr. Hun
tington in closing the interview by the remark, "We
have not disagreed over the closing of the gap.
There has been only a difference of business judg
ment."
Had the discussion brought out nothing more than
these declarations concerning the responsibility for
past delay, the subject would be of little importance,
notwithstanding its interest. It would have been no
more than a contribution to the history of the inner
councils of the Southern Pacific, and added nothing
to the public knowledge of the intention of the com
pany with respect to the time of beginning the work
on the long desired improvement.
Fortunately it did not end there. In his letter to
Mr. Ivison Mr. Huntington stated the railroad would
probably have the gap closed within a year after the
construction was fairly started, but he did not even
hint at the time when the work would begin. In his
interview with our correspondent in New York he
was more definite, and much more encouraging. He
is quoted as having said: "Recently Mrs. Stanford,
Mr. Crocker, Mr. Hubbard and myself met and con
sidered the advisability of closing the fifty-five mile
gap between the two town?. Mrs. Stanford and Mr.
Crocker gave their consent, and I at once ordered the
construction to proceed. I have given instructions
to push things vigorously, and I am hopeful the work
will be completed within a year."
In some respects this is the most important item of
news that has been given to Californians since the
year opened. It is of the highest value to all the
coast counties between San Francisco and Los An
geles, and indirectly to the State at large. The amount
of road to be laid is small, but the effect will be great,
for it will open a through line for the coast to the
south of us and thus prepare the way for the de
velopment of one of the most favored sections of the
State. Mr. Huntington has at last spoken words
that are truly encouraging, and now let us hope he
will live up to them.
WHO SUPPORTS BURNS?
OUR population is divided comprehensively into
two classes â drones and parasites, who feed on I
the State, and producers, who support it and !
add to its wealth, its power and its character. Dan ;
Burns is the candidate of the first class, who sustain
their laziness and their viciousness indirectly through
the railroad. A few tests, that each legislator can j
quickly apply, will prove this assertion. Has any
horticultural or agricultural community suggested
the election of Dan Burns? Have any miners or man- j
ufacturers, individually or collectively, mentioned him
as their choice? Have any merchants or mercantile I
bodies, bankers, capitalists or property-owners rallied
to his support? Have the schools, the universities, the
churches, any of the moral or intellectual forces of j
the community, manifested their preference for him? i
If all these questions are answered, as they necessarily :
must be. in the negative, what excuse is there for his
candidacy, and, still more, for his election?
Sports, gamblers, lobbyists, unclean politicians of
the lower type, the immoral and degrading pests of
our civilization, all impressed with the railroad brand,
are his impudent and blatant mercenaries. Which
combination do the members of the Legislature
recognize as the public or as composing the Repub
lican party, or as having the interests of the State at
heart? By which do they propose to be governed?
Their answer to these pertinent inquiries is loudly
demanded and will be received before the session
closes.
Either Packer Armour has been grievously lie-d
about or he owes this country immeasurable repara
tion, even to the erection of the tombstones marking
the resting-places of those who have gone hence
through eating canned germs of tuberculosis.
Stories that the Czar is a helpless invalid are now
said to be untrue. Nobody believed them anyhow,
and the correspondent is not entitled to more than
half space rates on his denial.
The evening bidders for railroad patronage a*Ve
scaling their bids very low down. Both are for
Burns.

DESTRUCTIVE SEA LIONS.
Thousands of Salmon Slaughtered in
the Upper Bay.
Deputy Fish Commissioner John Davis
has Just returned from a trip up the river
and reports that the spring run of salmon
has begun. It is true that these fish
have been ascending the rivers in small
numbers during the winter, but the big
run seems now to have started in. Around
Martinez the fishermen are complaining
loudly of the depredations of the sea lions
in the waters of the upper bay. These
creatures follow the salmon in great num
bers and in one day a good-sized, vigor
ous sea lion will destroy enough fish to
keep a canning factory running for half
that time. Aside from the destruction of
the fish, the fishermen have another rea-
BOU for complaint.
Many of the salmon get into the nets
and there they fall easy victims to the sea
lions. In the rush of these animals the
nets are literally torn to pieces and the
cost of repairing, aside from the loss of
fish, is no small feature in the profit and
loss account of the fishermen. As the sea
llon<* run as far as Sacramento and to
Bouldln Island in the San Joaquin, the net
men are all provided with rifles and shoot
whenever they see their aquatic enemies
fhow their he.ads above the surface of the
water.
Wells-Fargo Still Dodging.
The suit brought in the Superior Court
by ex-Attorney General Fitzgerald
against Well?. Fargo & Co. to compel
the corporation to pay the war revenue
tax on packages forwarded by it was
transferred yesterday from the Superior
Court to the United States Circuit Court.
Poolroom men are talking of making a
"book" on the proposition that the case
will not be decided before the war taxes
are abolished.

MUST HAVE CERTIFICATES.
Chinese Students and Merchants Not
to Be Landed Without Papers.
United States Circuit Judge Morrow, on j
behalf of the United States Circuit Court
of Appeals, handed down a decision yes- j
! terday reversing the decision of the Ore
! gon court in the case of the Government
apainst Chu Chi and Chu How.
The Chus are Chinese boys. 13 and 15
years old, respectively. They were al
lowed a landing on the ground that they
were students, notwithstanding the fact !
that they did not have a certificate to
that effect, as required by the statutes, j
The United States District Court in Ore- â 
gon decided that as the boys had proved
that they were students they were en- J
titled to land by reason of that fact !
alone and that the certificate was not i
necessary.
Judge Morrow's decision yesterday was
to the effect that the certificate is re
quired by the statute and that being with- i
i out the "certificate they had been landed j
i illegally. The fact that they were stu- I
i dents in fact did not establish their status
! under the law.
They will be deported.

find a law which says that a man who

SENATORSHIP-A. B. C. City. If the
Legislature should adiourn without elect
ing a United States Senator from Califor
nia there would be, as generally under
stood, a vacancy, the term of the incum
bent having expired on the 4th of March,
and the Governor would have the right to
appoint, the appointee holding until the
next session of the Lesislature, but in the
Oregon case there was a different ruling.
In that case the Legislature failed to elect
a Senator, the Governor appointed Cor
bett. but he was not accorded his seat, the
Senate holding that there was no va
cancy.

Baptist Church created a mild sensation

manner in which Protestant churches are
operated, erected and located, as com
pared with Catholic Institutions. He di

sons From the Catholics," was sufficient

its contents startled and amused his
brother ministers. In part he said:
Roman Catholics know how to build
churches. They do not put up shells or bams.
If you see a flimsy Catholic churcn you may
make up your mind It is a mission, to be re
placed In a few years by a splendid buiidine.
Anything may be good enough in wnlch to
make a beginning, but it is always re^aided
as a beginning. If they put up a frame or
patch up an old dwelling It is not ligardea as
a finished church. Some day they intend to
move cut. and amid weeping for vie old ana
Joy for the new, praise God in a splendid tem
ple that will stand to man and weather for
years. I am not raising the question wherÂ»

in their strength and majesty.
There 13 a glory in them we do n"t nn<i in

side they Inspire silence and devotion.

some cases and for work in others."

I grumble. 'If the circumstances change around

I put them in his button hole.
Dr Gibson then drew a fanciful picture

glass windows of a Catholic church and

i churches.
There is always a sign of life, some token
of enthusiasm that something is going on. The
Protestant church is always closed. The dust

The silent pUe of lumber suggests death, idle

LOST THIRTY DAYS IN A FOG
BARK WOOLAHAEA'S LONG VOY

Schooner Charles Hanson Pooped on
the Eureka Bar â Reinsurance
on Two Overdue Ships
Jumping.
The American ship M. P. Grace and the.
British bark Brussels have just com
': pl-eted two of the longest voyages, that
have been made between Australia and
' San Francisco In year?. Neither vessel
.. sighted the other during the run and yet
It would stem as though each captain
.was trying to be the last in. They sail
d within a day of each other, crossed the
equator the same day. 62 degrees of longi
tude apart and reached San Francisco
within forty-eight hours of each other.
Strange to say, the American ship en
countered nothing but light winds and
calms, while the British bark had "dirty
weather" nearly all the way and lost her
Jibboom In a squall.
The M. P. Grace left Sydney, N. S. W.,
on November 21 last. No southeast trade
winds wore picked up end the vessel
drifted nearly all the way to the equator,
â  which was crossed in 120 west longitude.
From the equator to port the Grace en
countered nothing but light winds and
â¢ calms.
The Brussels left Newcastle, N. S. TV.,
on November 20 last, and after getting off
shore had continuously dirty weather
; from the north and northeast to
latitude 5 south. The equator was cross- i
ed in 172 west longitude and ten days later |
â a squall struck the vessel, carrying away I
a number of sails, jibboom and fore top- !
gallant mast. The trade winds were lost i
in 19 north, after which the bark hud
nothing but light winds, accompanied by 'â¢
â¢â¢a. heavy cross sea. which made every one
of the crew long for port.
The Grace and Brussels were to long i
out that reinsurance was paid on both
of them. It was not heavy enough to i
tempt the gamblers, however.
The British bark woolahara, which ar- â¢
rived at Eureka the other day, gave her !
â  owners and agents a great scare. She
was forty-rive days out from Honolulu In
ballast and the insurance men were get
ting to wonder what had become of her. :
â After leaving Honolulu the bark made
. splendid time and in ten days was 400
â  miles- off Eureka. Then unusually heavy
weather was run into and the "Wo'olahara
was i riven back. She made the coast
again in a fog an.d for a month was off
the Eureka bar. Once or twice she tried
to- cross in. but had to stand out to sea ,
;again. Several times she heard the tugs !
.. whistle, but could net make herself heard '
.mi the fog. Finally the weather .beared j
,un. the bark was seen and towed into i
port. She will load 750.000 feet of lumber
for Sydney, X. S. W.
The schooner Charles Hanson, which
; left here over a week ago for Eureka,
had a hard time of it crossing in over the
Humboldt River bar. While being towed
in a big sea broke aboard, half killing two
â¢ of the crew and carrying away the taff
ra:l. The men were washed down the ;
companion way and had several bones I
broken. The cabin was flooded and the
cabin boy. who was laying the table for
di-nner. was half drowned. The Hanson
will load lumber for Hawaii.

BRIGHTS DISEASE
.
If you have any indication cf kidney trouble or a weak-
ness of these vital organs you may have Bright's disease, or

BATTLED WITH HEAD W/IINDS.

Francisco^in years has just been completed by the Brussels. She
sailed on November 20, and ever since has been fighting head
winds and calms. She and the M. P. Grace were trying to see which
would be the longest coming here. The Grace won.

A QUIET WEDDING.
Mr. Hoover and Miss Fresham Mar
ried After a Romantic Court
ship.
Ralph Y. Hoover and Miss Anna Fres
ham were quietly married at noon yester
day in the First Congregational Church
by the Rev. G. C. Adams. The ceremony
was witnessed by only a few intimate
friends of the couple. There is quite a
little romance connected with the mar
riage, over a year ago Miss Fresham,
with a friend, came to San Francisco
from Grand Rapids. Mich., in search of
health and pleasure. It was while she
was here that Mr. Hoover met her
It was the old story of love at first sight
and when Miss Fresham returned to her
ADVERTISEMENTS.

home some three months ago it was with
the understanding- that she would shortly
come back to California to make It her
home. Mr. Hoover is a prominent ma
chinist of this city, although he is a resi
dent of Napa. After a brief visit to the
home of his parents the young couple will
reside in Alameda.
Dora Fuhrig's Case.
The jury in whose hands the fate of the
notorious midwife. Dora Fuhrig. rests,
was dismissed yesterday afternoon by
Judge Cook that the admissibility of her
victim's dying statement might be ar
gued. Prior to her death Anna W.
Walmsley, for whose death Mrs. Fuhrig
is now on trial, made a statement that
Mrs. Fuhrig was responsible for her con
dition. Whether or not this statement
will be admitted will be decided by Judge
Cook this morning.

HOW THE RACES

COUGHS TRACK
Three of the Choices
in Front.
CALDWELL IN FORM AGAIN
LEFT EL SAX ADO AT THE
POST.
Tyro the Subject of a Presentation.
The Conditions Suited Him.
Survivor Downed Adolph
Spreckels.
Thfl Corrigan track furnished some
freckled-looking sport to a mild collection
of racegoers at Ingleslde yesterday. As
though the local populace could not get
along without them, a hurdle affair was
placed on the programme. Needless to
remark, it served as the means for Ed
Corrigan to add to his already badly
swollen list of winnings. Seventeen
bookmakers cut in, and there was busi
ness enough for about half that number.
Three favorites strutted past the wire in
front.
About the grandest piece of barrier
work Mr. Caldwell has performed in some
time he reserved for reopening day. In
the five-furlong dash, fourth on the card.
Corrigan started Maxello and It was to
have oeen the "great big thing" of the
afternoon. The Maxim horse closed
equal favorite with El Salado. The lat
ter, with Piggott up. coijld have won
hands down, but unfortunately in his de
sire to get Maxello away, Caldwell left
El SaUdo at the post. If ever anything
looked deliberate, this did. Piggott was
ordered to take his mount back, and
while facing the reverse way, was left, to
gether with February, another dangerous
one. As it turned out, Maxello was not
among the first three. Don Luis, a 20 to 1
shot, led Al out over a length. To make
the play forcible. Piggott was suspended
by the starter for the balance of the
week.
The capture of the mile and a quarter
hurdle race was a ridiculous piece of
work made more absurd by its crudity.
Tyro, the Corrigan entry, was allowed to
win by himself. The gelding might havo
been pounds the best, but others took no
chances.
A field of ten two-year-olds lined up in
the opening three and a half furlong
scramble. At post time W. L. Stantield"s
Apache filly, Kitty Kelly, had a slight
call in the betting, and though she made
a handsome showing, was beaten out a
head by Morbid, the entry of Walter Jen
nings, ridden by Spencer. The winner
opened a 2 to 1 favorite, receding later to
7to 2. Montebank finished in the show.
Garnet Ferguson's recent purchase.
Dare 11. closed favorite for the mile ami
a sixteenth, but took no part of the
money. Eddie Jone* hustled old Tom
Cromwell out the first part, killing the
favorite off. In the run home Cromwell
had it all his own way, winning easily

In the Divorce Courts.
Josephine Winthrop was granted a di
vorce yesterday from her husband, the
notorious O. W. AVinthrop. who was sen
tenced to life imprisonment for kidnaping
Millionaire Campbell of Honolulu. The
divorce was granted on the ground that
Winthrop had been convicted of a felony.
Mary Clark secured a divorce from Hiram
A. Clark on the ground of willful neglect.
Suits for divorce on the ground of deser
tion have been filed by Philip J. Bailey
against Flora F. Bailey and Katherine
T. Benjamin against Arthur T. Benjamin.

EARLY CROPS DAMAGED.
KFFECT OF THE RECENT COLD SPELL ON
THE MARKET.
The New York Commercial says:
"The effects of the cold wave upon all de
scriptions of early Southern produce are be
coming felt more and more every day. They
are chiefly noticeable In the continued scarcity
and bad condition in which a great part or j
the vegetables reach the distributors here.
â¢There has been a scarcity of early peas,
eggplants cabbage, tomatoes and strawberries,
nor do the later reports of the damage done in
the more northerly section of the South indi
cate that these conditions will improve. Grow
ing crops that had not reached maturity before
the cold wave struck them have been ruined
either completely or partially. special bulletin
â â¢In Georgia, according to a special bulletin
compiled from reports of several hundred cor
respondents scattered throughout the State the
damage will amount to millions of dollars
Peaches in the lower counties were heavily
damaged and other fruits suffered conspicuous
>: 'In South Carolina and North Carolina the
damage to immature vegetables was very se
vere and produce dealers here believe that
most crops will be very short and that prices
will rule higher on early produce than has been
the cape for several years."
FRUIT AND SALMON IN ENGLAND.
Callfornlan fruits are moving off briskly at
firm rates, says the London Grocers' Gazette,
and supplies on the spot will be augmented by
4>> C6O cases which have -arrived by fhe Pampa
this week. Practically the whole of these
fruits have been -old to arrive. The Marie
Hackfeld. which is advised as having arrived
at Liverpool, brings 82.461 cases. There has
been rather more activity in the salmon mar- â 
ket this week and Alaska Salmon has In some
cases-been held for an advance of 6d per case.
The Pampa has arrived with 15,192 cases of
Salmon from San Francisco, and the Marie
Hackfeld is advised as having arrived at_Llv
erpool with 89,921 cases, also from San Fran
cisco.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. March 6.â Prices dropped
steadily to-day on somewhat restricted deal
ings, each rally affording the bears another
level from -which to attack prices. With some
exceptions leading chares opened below Sat
urday's close. London sold some 12.000 shares
on balance and arbitrage houses made pur
chases In London before our opening and Bold
here. Speculation was purely professional and
much was made of the firmness of money,
which at one time touched ZVi per cent for call
loans, at which price it closed. The "best au
thorities" were quoted as turning the money
flurry as only a flash in the pan. The Im
provement in the demand for money In the
dry goods and other lines of trade, together :
with the preparations to finance the Central
Pacific settlement with the Government, the
Chicago and Alton deal and some Industrial
combinations, were responsible for . the im
proved tone. It was argued that a sustained
advance from current rate 3 would be fol
lowed by gold imports, but It was pointed out
that many Interior banks have supplies of
money above their previous records. Of the
g-old on the way from the Pacific Coast, which
failed to arrive in time to figure in the bank
statement, a round amount arrived to-day.
The anthracite coal shares, which were strong
in the early dealings, eased off sympathetical
ly with the general market except for Dela
ware, Lackawanna and Western, which in the â¢
final dealings ran up over 8 points to 162^.
Chicago, Indianapolis and Louisville preferred
was very Strong, going up over 5 points on
rumors that the ("mine dividend micht be
larger than had been expected. General Elec
tric was strong also In the early part of the
day going up 3Va to 120, part of which it sub
sequently lost. It was said that the com
pany had secured some new contracts and
that the earnings were improving. Sugar was
rather steady most of the day on the advance
in the refined article, but fell off in the late
dealings. Some realizing appeared in Amer
ican Tobacco, which broke some 5 points after
an initial rise. The grangers were Inclined to
heaviness, with Burlington making a net loss
of 2%. Most stocks showed considerable looses
and the close was weak. To-day's bond trad
ire was at the expense of values, many of
the active issues giving way smartly. Total
pales. $3,130,000. 'â â  '
United States old 4e registered declined % In
the bid price.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. March The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were inactive and dull on
the eve of the settlement beginning to-morrow.
Americans opened firm above parity, to the
surprise of many onlookers, but gradually
j weakened. There was a small rally on New
! York buying of coalers, assisted by a private
cable dispatch to the effect that the anthracite
coal deal was arranged, but later New York
sold freely, causing a very flat close.
London fears money troubles over some of
I the recently established trusts, but has no
| commitment, long or short.
Spanish 4s touched 66%. and closed 55%.
Copper was a pound down. The shares are
heavy. Tintos were 3*4, and closed 3v,Â»- Utahs
and Bostons were practically unsalable at 7%@
28, respectively.

THE STOCK MARKET.
On the morning session of the Bond Ex
change the loading fluctuation was an ad
vance in Mutual Electric to $16. Business was
good. Afternoon quotations were unchanged.
Mining stocks opened lower, owing to the
continued delay In the pumping operations.
The telegram from the pump read as follows:
"Water twenty-nine feet four inches below
1750-foot level. The pump will not be started
until some time Wednesday, as the Risdon
people wish to be present. They will arrive
to-morrow." The weekly report appears be
low.
In the afternoon the feeling was firmer and
prices advanced to a point generally above the
best figures of Saturday.
The Ophlr Mining Company has levied an as
sessment of 15 cents ocr share, delinquent
April 10.
The stockholders of the Standard Consoli
dated Mining Company have authorized a re
duction of the capital from $20,01*>.000, divided
into 200,000 shares of the par value of $lÂ»0
each, to $2,000,000. divided Into 200,000 shares of
$10 each.
A special meeting of the stockholders of the
Syndicate Mining Company of Bodle has been
called for May 10. to authorize a reduction
of the capital stock from $10,000,000. divided
Into 100,000 shares of the par values of $10u
each, to $100,000, divided into 100,000 -shares
of the par value of $1 each.
The Mead Gold Mining and Milling Company
paid Its second quarterly dividend at the rate
of 10 per cent, or 40 per cent per annum, on
March 2.
The Boston and California Gold Mining and
Milling Company paid a regular quarterly divi
dend at the rate of 6 per cent per annum on
March 2.
The official report of General Manager Ross
of the Comstock Pumping Association for the
past week Is as follows:
During the past week pumping at the C.
and C. shaft has been interrupted twice by de
fective discharge pipeâ tapered section, ten
feet long, made of '4-lnch steel, riveted and
connected to the elevator and the 14-lnch dis
charge pipe with heavy cast-Iron flanges, the
steel of which was probably injured in roll
ing. This broken section is to be replaced
with a 4-foot section of phosphor-bronze, that
will be connected directly with the seamless
discharge column. The water has been low
â¢<Ted fifty-three feet below the 1750 level sta
tion, or eighty-one feet below the discharge,
leading to the Sutro tunnel. The only place
where the action of the pumps can be noted
in the adjoining mines at present is at the
'"hollar combination shaft. At this point we
find a slight lowering of the water, It having
fallen ten inches since we started pump
ing at the C. and C. shaft. The Chollar com
bination shaft is located 3300 feet south and
1000 feet east of the C. and C: shaft. The
slight fall of ten Inches is rather a small
matter to call attention to. but as the meas
urements were carefully made there Is no
doubt but in continuing the pumping at the
C. and C. shaft we will drain a large section
of the Comstock lode.
We expect to replace the. defective pipe with
the phosphor-bronze section in a few days.
We have cleaned out, retimbered and laid
track in the 1400-foot level drift a distance of
seventy-two feet on account of the Consolidat
ed California and Virginia Mining Company.
During the week we have completed 500 feet
of the Osblston shaft connection to accom
modate the Sutro tunnel cars, on account of
the Gould & Cury and Best & Belcher mining
CO For aT and on account of the Savage Mining
Company we continue cleaning out their con
necting drift with the main Sutro tunnel. As
mentioned last week, this work is done for
the purpose of enabling them to catch up
and convey such water as is now flowing Into
the lower levels to the tunnel drain boxes.
The report from the Consolidated California
and Virginia mine for the past week states
that the C. and C. shaft for fifty feet below
the 1750 level has been cleaned out and re
paired and the pump rod column, piping ana
Stays have been taken out of the pump com
partment. From the 1750 level down this com
partment will be repaired so that the pump
cage can be used for all work on the pump-
Ing plant after it has been installed, as dur
ing the installation they have to use the cen
ter hoist compartment. On the 1400 level the
drift has been cleaned out and retimbered and
track laid for a total distance of 264. feet.
Work has been resumed on the 1750 station
and has been continued on the electric In
-8I n the Ophlr mine during the past week
the old workings cut into by the north drift
on the Central tunnel level were examined
and the quartz vein they had been following
was found to make a sharp turn to the east
and that it had been followed by a drift on
the hanging wall side for 150 feet, where the
"drift had been filled. At the point of recent
connection there Is a west drift extending 200
feet into the footwall. They are now pre
paring to Vun three crosscuts into the foot
wall at distances of fifty feet apart On the
Sutro tunnel level connection work has been
started and the drift from the north lateral
has been advanced five feet.
In the Sierra Nevada mine the northwest
drift on the 140-foot level was advanced last
week thirty-five feet; total length 165 feet.
The face is In porphyry and quartz. The south
drift from the top of the upraise eighty feet
above the 900 level Is out ninety-three feet.
The face is in clay and porphyry.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographtc
OffW, located at the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete pets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
liehts, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating: whether the ball was dropped
on time or ptving the error, if any. Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
W. B. HUGHES.
Lieutenant. U. 8. X., in charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the rarly morning tides are given In the left
hand column, and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column Rives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tldÂ»
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the cj^y. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

RISING WATERS
Floods in Kentucky and
Ohio.
LOWLANDS ARE INUNDATED
FAMILIES FORCED TO LEAVE
THEIR HOMES.
Streams Are Still Rising and It Is
Feared That Former High-
Water Marks Will
Be Passed.
Special Dispatch to ThÂ© Call.
CINCINNATI, March 6.â The Ohio
River at this point had reached a stage
'. of 53 feet 9 inches at 10 o'clock to-night
| and was risinjg at the rate of three
; inches an hour. But little damage has
I ' been done here, though many business
â  men in the lowlands are removing their
; goods from cellars. A sixty-foot stage
of water would drive some business
: llrms located near the river into second
j stories, but railroad trains would not
! bo prevented running regularly. Up the
river, at Portsmouth, many families
have been driven from their homes, and
several factories have been flooded,
. causing suspension of operations.
LOUISVILLE, Ky., March 6.â The
! swollen streams in Kentucky are caus
'. Ing. great damage, especially to farm
! ers. It is feared the worst is yet to
! come, as the streams are still rising.
i The Kentucky Kiver is rising rapidly
1 and much of the bottom lands are al
-1 ready inundated. All the tributary
streams are badly swollen and a rec
t ord-breaker is feared.
Besides the damage to property sev
i eral casualties have resulted already.
J. W. Gordon was drowned near Frank
! fort while trying to ford Stoney Creek.
| William Abshire, near Richmond; Ja
! cob Ewing, at Ewing Station, and Neal
â¢ Carter, near Mt. Sterling, lost their
; lives.
At Cattlettsburg the Government
gauge is 54.6 feet, and the river is ris
ing slowly. Merchants and residents
on Front and Center streets, near Catt
letts Creek, are moving to higher
ground. The water is now 18 inches
[ above the danger line. Much damage
i ta reported up the Big Sandy River. At
I Louisville the river is 54 feet and still
i rising. It is the highest water since
1562.
PORTSMOUTH, Ohio, March 6.â The
river has swept over the lower part of
i the city and residents have fled, to
I higher ground. The rise last night was
â  rapid, and business on Front street was

An Inmate of a Foundlings' Home
Burned to Death.
SACRAMENTO. March 6.â Myrtle Rob
erts, a nve-year-old girl, met a most hor
rible death at the Foundlings' Home last
night. The girl had been playing with
matcheÃ in the playroom of the home, of
which she was an Inmate, in the afternoon
and her dress accidentally caught fire.
When the matron reached the scene the
child was running around the room fran
tic with pain. The flames were extin

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MARCH 7, 1899.
ESCAPES FROM
A PESTHOUSE
Smallpox Patient Causes
a Scare.
EXCITEMENT AT SPOKANE
MOB PURSUES A SUPPOSED
CRIMINAL.
Scatters in Wild Alarm When Told
the Fugitive Is a Man Made
Insane by a Dread
Disease.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, March 6.â A special from
Spokane reports that Archie Brown, a
former musician of the Frank Daniels
Company, created a sensation in that
city to-day by escaping from a pest
house, where he had been imprisoned
because of being afflicted with small
pox. Later, finding himself on crowded
thoroughfares, he was taken for a fugi
tive criminal and chased by. a mob
composed of hundreds of people.
The Daniels company played at Spo
kane last week. Brown became ill on
Saturday and developed a genuine case
of smallpox. IJe became delirious yes
terday, broke out of the pesthouse, and.
boarding a crowded car, rode to the city.
The conductor believing Brown to be
crazy telephoned to the police, who
went in search of the afflicted man.
In the meantime Brown had left the
car, rushed up and down the crowded
streets, made himself troublesome in a
half dozen big stores, scared women
and children, and when found by the
poliro wa^: surrounded by hundreds of
people, who believed him to be a crim
inal.
Brown had made several efforts to
escape, but each time the crowd had
grabbed him and felled him to the
street. Then a physician arrived and
pronounced thp man a victim of small
pox and the hundreds of people scat
tered in every direction, each satisfied
that he could discover germs of the
disease on. his person.
The Health Officer advised a general
vaccination, but it was .found that the
pupply of vaccine points was limited;
Coast cities were immediately wired to
for a supply. A general scare prevails
to-night at Spokane, but there is be
lieved to be little danger of the pest
spreading.
UNION SAVINGS BANK
DIRECTORS TO RESIGN
Agreement Reached After a Confer
ence With Attorney Gen
eral Ford.
SAN JOSE, March 6.â The new board of
directors of the wrecked Union Savings
Bank to-night, by a vote of 4 to 3, decided
to resign in a body.
Attorney General Ford, Deputy G. A.
Sturtevant and Bank Commissioner Bar
rett came down this afternoon to meet
the directors. The Attorney General ad
vised that all the directors resign, as they
had no legal Standing now and had no
right to elect members since the failure.
This suggestion met the approval of Di
rectors Wright, Eaton, Jones and Cal
lisch. James W. Rea and C. P. Braslan
opposed it and Director Bowdcn refused
to vote.
To-morrow Attorney General Ford will
file a supplemental complaint in the suit
recently brought to have the bank de
clared insolvent. In this he will aver the
directors have all resigned and ask the
court to fill vacancies in the directory.
This will be answered by the board ad
mitting the fact. Then the appointment
of directors will come. A long list of
namesâ among them those of all the old
and new directors â will be submitted to
Judge Lorigan for selection. The stock
holders and depositors will again take a
hand and try to obtain recognition. They
will oppose the appointment of any of the
old directors, on the ground that bank
wreckers should not be allowed to handle
a bank's business.
As yet no real work In the settlement
of the bank's affairs has commenced, and
the depositors are growing restless. They
will meet on Thursday, and it' is said
steps will be taken then to rush a settle
ment.

BRIBERY CHARGE
WAS NOT PROVED
Majority Report to Utah
Legislature.
M'CUNE FOUND GUILTLESS
MINORITY SUBMITS A DISSENT
ING OPINION.
Declares That the Senatorial Candi
date Did Seek to Obtain
Votes by the Use of
Money.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SALT LAKE, March 6.â The Legis
lative Investigating Committee sub
mitted a majority and minority report
to the joint assembly this afternoon.
The majority report is signed by Sena
tor Whitney and Representatives Stew
art, Mansfield, Sorensen and Cura
mings. The minority report is signed
by Senators Shurtliff and Howells. The
llndings of the- majority report are as
follows:
1. In the matter of the charge of brib
ery preferred by Representative Law
against A. \\". McCune we Jind, after the
careful consideration, that the
charge is not sustained by the evidence.
2. In the Sloan-Nebeker case we find
that the said ft. AY. Sloan improperly ap
proached Senator W. G. NVbeker for the
purpose of securing his (Nebeker's) sup
port for Mr. McCune, but the evidence
oot establish an attempted bribery
or other public offense.
3. In the I vms- Jackson case the evi
dence is not sufficient to justify the con
clusion that any attempt was made to
secure by improper means Mr. Jackson'a
vote. We believe that in this case Mr.
Ja^-ks^n labored under an honest but mis
taken impression.
4. The evidence in the Cook-Farr mat
ter Is so remote that we deem it irrele
vant to any issue under consideration.
The Lapish charge â a supplemental case
âIs one in which the evidence bearing
upon the statement made is not sufficient
to warrant the finding of an attempted
bribery or other public offense.
In conclusion, we record our emphattc
disapproval of the present method of con
ducting Senatorial campaigns, particular
ly the practice by Senatorial candidates
of opening headquarters, employing num
erous assistants and entertaining by
means of free dinners, free theaters and
other gratuitous dispensations, persons
whose votes are desired by me candidates
at whose expense those things are pro
vided. It not only tends to evil, but it
works evil and In the interest of good
government, pure elections and that
higher political morality which all good
citizens desire to see attained, the prac
tice should be abolished. We -also con
demn the circulation of unfounded ru
mors designed to injure the prospects of
Senatorial candidates.
The specific finding in the minority
report is that A. W. McCune did, by
the use of money, attempt to secure the
vote of Albert A. Law, and that R. W.
Sloan sought by improper means to se
cure the vote of State Senator W. G.
Nebeker for Mr. McCune for United
States Senator.
After the reading of the reports the
joint aster bly adjourned until 9:30 to
morrow morning, when the reports will
be taken up for discussion.

GAINS NEW MEMBERS
Santa Clara County Exchange Joins

tion.
PAN JOSE, March 6.â The Pacific Coast
Fruit Association, organized here a short
time ago for the purpose of handling the
prune crop, is being encouragingly re
â ! all Over the State. To-day a large
! ly attended! nn:Â«'ting of prune-growers be
< longing to the Santa Clara County Fruit
Exchange voted unanimously to join the
ni\v association. The meeting was an
enthusiastic one. President. Philo Ilersey
presided. D. M. Delmas explained the
objects of the new organization, and tne
discussion that followed showed the unan
imity of the growers. It was decided to

is the largest handler of dried fruits on

county, such as the West Side, Berry
eSsa, East Side and Campbell Fruit
Unions, will follow it. All tries*? organ
izations will abide by the price and rules

HITT TALKS OF
LAWS IN HAWAII
Resultof Congress' Fail
ure to Act,
SITUATION NOT HOPELESS
EMBARRASSMENTS, HOWEVER,
ARE LIKELY TO ARISE.
One Great Source of Difficulty Will
Be the Failure to Properly
Determine Admiralty
Questions.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
WASHINGTON, March 6.
There has been considerable specula
tion as to the effect upon the conditions
In the Hawaiian Islands owing to the
failure of Congress to enact the legis
lation demanded.
Representative Hitt of Illinois, chair
man of the Committee on Foreign Af
fairs In the last Congress, who was one
of the Hawaiian commissioners, is
probably as conversant with Hawaiian
affairs as any public man in Washing
ton. Mr. Hitt was asked to-day for an
expression of opinion upon what may
be expected in Hawaii between this
time and the reassembling of Congress
â¢and the enactment of proper laws.
"I am by no means inclined to take a
hopeless view of the situation," said
Mr. Hitt. "It Is true that embarrass
ments will arise. For instance,, a strict
construction of the existing law, or
rather absence of law, would permit
criminals to go free because of the con
flict between the United States laws
and local regulations. But it is not to
be considered that any such construc
tion will be placed upon the case. The
courts will see to it that public order
is conserved.
"Another great source of difficulty
will be in the determination of admiral
ty questions. As a matter of fact, they
will not be determined, but the people
will have to worry along as best they
can. As for customs questions, I think
the President will have sufficient pow
er, in the absence of specific legislation,
to settle them.
"I believe that when the next Con
gress convenes it will be possible to se
cure consideration for the Hawaiian
bill and to enact a comprehensive law
for the islands. In the meantime, the
ability and integrity of the men in
charge of the government will be a suf
ficient safeguard for the interests of the
people." .

Mining Claims Bonded.
REDDING, March 6.â lt is reported on
the authority of one of the owners that
a Toup of seventeen claims In tlie sul
phide zone of the Backbone mining dis
trict has been bonded for $130,000. A pay
ment of $30,000 must be made by the 25th
of March, the remainder within a year.
Three San Francisco mining m.en have
taken the bond. The bonded property Is
owned jointly by Fred Bowler, W. H.
Bartley, Arthur Baker, John Monahan
and Fred Simonds.
Advance in Price of Sugar.
NEW YORK, March 6.â An all-round
advance in the price of refined sugars was
made to-day by the American Sugar Re
fining Company. . Arbuckle Bros, and
Howell, Son & Co., the last named firm
representing the independent refiners.
The' advance is M\ cent a pound, bringing
barrel sugar up to aVi cents and package
sugar to 5Vi cents. The Mollenhauer re
finory, one of the independent concerns,
resumed operations to-day.

YOUNG WIFE
SEEKS DEATH
WITH POISON
Manimco Jealous of
His Friend.
HE RETURNED IN A HURRY
FOUND HIS WIFE CALLING ON A
BOOMER.
to Kill Her, but Dropped
thÂ« Knife When She De
clared That She Wm
Innocent.
Oakland ' Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March. 6.
IClttte 'Manimco wants to die. Her hus
band is jealous of her, and this morning i
took up a knife, threatening to end their j
troubles. The little woman protested her j
Innocence, dared him to strike and he I
threw away the knife. Then she went
home, swallowed half an ounce of sul
phate of zinc and tried to die.
The cause of all the Manimco troubles is |
a mutual friend of the family, Vincent !
Margio, who visited the couple last night
and was invited to stay until morning.'
Early this morning Manimco left his j
home In the Rosalie House and went to :
Jus restaurant in the Sj.. Lawrence House, i
About half an hour later he suddenly re
turned. His own rooms were vacant, and
he heard his wife talking to Margio in
another room. He reached all sorts of |
conclusions, and at once determined that j
eomebody must die.
"I really want to die," said Mrs.
Manimco. who is about 25 years old, "be
cause my husband is unreasonably Jealous !
end accuses me of all sorts of things
without cause. This morning when be
went out 1 got up and dressed and went
to the room Margio occupied. I was talk- j
Ing to him when my husband returned. I
He at once suspected that I was unfaith
ful to him, and without giving me an op- j
potunlty to say a word he went back to
the restaurant. I followed him, and when
I got there he seized me roughly, and, |
raising" a butcher knife, he swore he j
would kill me. I said, "Kill me; I am â 
lnnocenx. " and then he dropped the knife. I
I returned to my room and swallowed the j
poison. 1 felt so bad I wanted my little i
daughter to take some, but she ran down- ;
etairs and told my husband that I had j
taken poison. He tame up. and I was so j
anxious to die that 1 denied taking any I
poison and he believed me and went ,
away. Shortly afterward my daughter
found me on the floor, and then ran for j
eman, who took me to the hospital, j
â¢ been married to Manimco but three !
months, and was a widow when I met
Mrs. Manimco was attended to promptly '
will not die unless she takes more j
poison. Her husband says that a divorce j
only thing that will satisfy him. i
Altogether it wus an unlucky day in the j
restaurant, for while Manimco was up
braiding his wife his chief cook, James F. i
Lane, fell heavily and fractured his thigh,
and he was also taken to the hospital.
It is f.'ared a tragedy will result lrom
the quarrel between Manimco, Margio and
the former's wife, for the Italian colony
has taken a general interest in it, as the
restaurant man is a leading spirit among
his couhtrymen.
SUDDEN DEATH
OF A CANDIDATE
DB. J. P. McCABGAB WAS A PBO
HIBITION LEADEB,
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
Broadway,' March 7.
Dr. J. P. McCargar died suddenly at
his residence this noon. He has been ill
for a few days with erysipelas, but no
one anticipated a fatal result. A few
days ago he was apparently quite well
and attending to business.
Dr. McCargar has been practicing his
profession In Oakland many years, and
has always been an active worker in tem
perance movements. For a long time
he was editor of the Pacific Prohibition
ist, and has frequently been a candidate |
on Prohibition tickets. Four years ago
he was the candidate for Mayor and his
name will be on the ticket to be voted
next Monday as the candidate for City i
Treasurer.
He leaves a widow and six children.
A SECBET MABBIAGE.
Lulu Homeier Only Announced Her
Wedding After Long Delay.
Alameda Office San Francisco Call,
1428 Park street, March 6. ;j
Miss Lulu Homeier, youngest daughter
of Louis Homeier, the well-known musi
cian, formerly leader of the Baldwin The
ater orchestra, stated to-day that she had
been secretly married October 24, 1897, to
George C. Shane of 2714 Sutter street, San
j Francisco. Immediately after divulging
I her secret the young lady disappeared
and her parents are ignorant of her
' â whereabouts.
f Mrs. HomJtler, the girl's mother, stated
this evening that she knew nothing about
the marriage and was apparently pained
at the strange actions of her daughter.
"Lulu left home Saturday night." said
Mrs. Homeier, "stating that she had se
cured employment at 564 Tenth street,
Oakland. I have not seen her since. The
announcement of her wedding is news to
me and I can hardly believe that it is
true." â¢
According to the story told by Miss Ho
meier she was married in San Rafael Oc
tober 24, 1897. by a Rev. Mr. Dodge. Imme
diately after the ceremony the bride re
turned to her home in this city and kept
her secret for nearly a year and a half.
Her husband, who is employed as a book
' keeper, returned to his duties. Since then,
it is stated he has been a regular visit
or at the Homeier home, but neither of
the young folks gave the slightest inti
mation that they were married. Miss Ho
meier said the reason for keeping the
marriage a secret, was the fear of paren
tal wrath.
A week after the San Rafael ceremony
Mr. and Mrs. Shane attended a reception
given in Alameda by Dr. Alfred Sander,
who figured prominently in the Roebke-
Green /engagement. For more than two
years previous Miss Homeier and Dr.
Sander had been fast friends and it was
currently reported that they were en
gaged. .
Since the story of the young lady's mar
riage became. public many of her friends
called to mind that she had told them
early in 1898 that she was married, but in
view of the rumor connecting her name
â with that of Dr. Sander they considered
her statements as a joke, hut as they say
in the books, it is no joke when people
really get married.

Mrs. Crawford's Death. .
OAKLAND March. Coroner Mehr
mann and a jury Inquired into the death
of Mrs Lucy B. Crawford this evening
and found that death was caused by
shock, due to accidental burns sustained
by the explosion of a coal oil stove at her

tive or Massachusetts, aged 49 years.
THE HAND OF
THE LAW ON
COLLECTOR PECK
Santa Cruz Ex-Offi
cial in Trouble.
HE IS CHARGED WITH FELONY
BAID TO HAVE EMBEZZLED PUB
LIC FUNDS.
He Says He Resigned His Office Last
August, Is Innocent and
a Victim of His
Enemies,
ALAMEDA, March 6.â W. E. Peck, ex-
Tax and License Collector for Santa Cruz
County, was arrested here to-night on
warrants charging him with felony em-
bezzlement. The charges are the out
growth of his dealings with Santa Cruz
County and are based on indictments
found by the Grand Jury. Although he
was arrested early in the evening, the
police did everything possible to keep the
matter a secret. Peck has been on a
spree and stopping at a Park street lodg
ing house in company with a woman.
Immediately after Peck was taken into
custody the woman disappeared, and if
the police know anything of her identity
they will not acknowledge it. Peck was
eating his dinner in a local restaurant
in company with his female companion
when Marshal Conrad served the warrant
of arrest.
When Peck was seen in the tanks to
night he said he would not have been in
Alameda if he had not been taken sick
while coming through here on the narrow
gauge train from Saji Jose to San Fran
cisco en route to Santa Cruz, where he
proposed to go to face his accusers. He
would not state why he had not gone di
rectly home from San Jose, that being
the shortest route.
"I am Innocent of any wrong, said the
prisoner, "and the report of the county
expert will show that my accounts were
straight. I resigned my office last August
and remained In Santa Cruz until two
months ago. when I went to Tulare
County on a mining expedition. A\ hile
I was away the Supervisors, who had it in
for me, managed to get the Grand Jury
to indict me on some purely technical
charges. That is all there is to the mat
ter lam not Indebted to Santa Cru*
County one cent and will willingly po
back to face my accusers. The woman I
was with at the time I was arrested is
an Alamedan, but I refuse to give her
name." _ â¢ â¢ _ ...
The Sheriff of Santa Cruz County will
take Peck home to-morrow.
DR. WOOLSEY'S ALLEGATIONS
CONSIDERED UNFOUNDED
HIS OWN EXPERT PROVES HIM
TO BE IN ERROR.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
â¢ 908 Broadway, March 6.
E. R. Jackson, the artist and photog
rapher who furnished District Attorney
Allen with the photographs of the dead
Lillian Brandes showing minutely every
bruise, mark and discoloration on her
body, was vindicated this afternoon, and
that by F. A. Webster, another photog
rapher, whom Dr. E. H. Woolsey had
called in as an expert.
The allegations made by Dr. Woolsey,
chief expert for the defense in the
Brandes murder trial, to the effect that
the photographs or negatives had been
tampered with, or "doctored," were clear
ly proved to be unfounded, and the pride
of the aged doctor In his self-confidence
and infallibility for accuracy and correct
ness sustained a severe jolt.
.Mr. Webster very carefully scrutinized
the negatives and compared them with
the photographs. When he had concluded
his work he stated that he was unable to
find a blemish or foreign mark on the
negatives, also that the photographs were
good prints from the negatives.
Dr Woolsey was apparent4y not pleased
with the result, and moved to leave the
room but Mr. Jackson intercepted.
"Dr. Woolsey," said the young artist,
"before you go I want you to understand
that your expert, Mr. Webster, has seen
and examined this entire lot of negativ.-s.
a~d has found no blemishes or spots other
than those that should be there. You un
derstand he says that the photos are good
prints from the negatives?"
"Well, that's goodâ that's good. re
plied Dr. Woolsey, his cheeks flushed with
a sort of what of it blush. "In fact I did
not absolutely claim they were 'doctored.
I guess you exploited that fact in the pa
pers yourself." .
But Artist Jackson would not down and
close "You told me yourself, in the
presence of reporters and witnesses, that
these pictures and negatives were 'doc
tored.' I guess you have forgotten all you
The murder trial will be resumed to
morrow and there is little doubt that the
correctness of the pictures will again be
questioned. Dr. Crowley or Dr. Pratt
will probably be the first witness called
on the part of the defense.
TANNERY IN OAKLAND
BURNED TO THE GROUND
HEAVY LOSS TO J. S. DEBBY &
SONS.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway. March 6.
The largre tannery of J. S. Derby & Sons
on East Twelfth street, near Eighteenth
avenue, was completely destroyed by fire
at about 6 o'clock this morning. The ftrra
estimates the total loss on stock. Includ
ing 2000 hides, and plant at $16,000, cov
ered by an insurance of $4000.
The blaze was first discovered by H. H.
Lewis, residing at 1126^ East Fourteenth
street, and he turned in the alarm. When
the fire. apparatus arrived there was .left
but little hope of saving the building, and
most of the Fire Departments attention
was directed with effect to saving the
tannery of Brown & Adams, situated di- j
rectly east of the blaze. A cottage just |
opposite the burning building was
scorched considerably, but was saved by
the chemical engines.
The cause of the fire is thus far un
known There was no one about the place
at the time so far as known, and there
had been no fire in the'furnaces since last
Friday. It is believed, however, that
tramps unintentionally set the place on
fire. , , Â _ ,
Injured by the Local.
OAKLAND, March 6.â John Norton, a
painter, employed by Al Woods, while
driving In a-wagon at the railroad cross
ing near Golden Gate was struck by the
7 o'clock Berkeley local to-night and
severely though not believed fatally, in
jured. He was treated by Dr. Rowe at
the Receiving Hospital and it required
sixteen stitches to sew up his wounds.
His escape from death was miraculous.
His horse was killed and the wagon de
molished.
Injured by a Falling Pole.
ALAMEDA. March 6.â E. Luckhardt. an
employe of the electric light company,
was seriously injured this evening "by the
falling of a pole on which he was engaged
stringing wire. The accident happened
near Kruitvale. Luckhardt v,as near the
top when the pole snapped, throwing him
to the ground and falling across his body.
His wrist and shoulder were dislocated
and it Is feared that he has sustained
Internal injuries. He was brought to the
home of hia brother-in-law, G. A, Weise,
in this city.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MARCH 7, 1899.
CANNOT USE HIS
GOOD RIGHT ARM
Fitzsimmons Crippled
Temporarily.
WARNED BY HIS PHYSICIANS
TOLD HE MUST NOT FIGHT, BUT
BEFUSES TO OBEY.
The Champion's Elbow Injured by a
Blow Delivered by Kenney,
His Big Sparring
Partner.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, March 6.â Something is
radically wrong with Robert Fitzsim
mons' right elbow. He cannot Btraight
en his mighty right arm, and when
anybody touches him on the "funny
| bone" the champion groans in agony.
i Two doctors examined Fitzsimmons to
i day, and with the aid of an "X-ray"
made a careful study of the injured
elbow. They told him he needed abso
lute rest for at least two months and
â  warned him against engaging in any
j pugilistic encounters for some time.
When Fitzsimmons heard the verdict
of the medical men he simply said:
"This won't stop me from fighting
I 'Jim' Jeffries. I will go into the ring
i with him if I have only one good hand,
! and I'll win out at that."
I According to Drs. McNamara and
j Sincere the pugilist is suffering from a
i "displacement," or chipping-off of the
i tip of the olecranon process. When
: asked how he hurt it Fitzsimmons said:
"About four months ago I was box
j ing with my sparring partner, big
; 'Yank' Kenney. In blocking one of his
I heavy swings I caught the full force of
I the blow on the point of the right el
bow. It was a funny place to get it.
; but my arm dropped like a shot and
i the pain was something awful. I tried
massages and liniments, and after a
time managed to get the swelling down,
but I haven't been able to straighten
my arm since the accident. When I
try to do it the pain drives me frantic."
It was found a sliver had been
chipped from the bone, which causes an
irritation and constant inflammation of
the muscles.
Class Bush Barely Averted.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, March 6.â
The sophomore class baseball team to-day
won the second victory by defeating the
freshman nine. The score was 12 to 7.
The sophs will next come up against the
junior team, when the interclass cham
pionship will be decided.
Something of the old-time spirit which
existed in the class organizations before
the rule against rushing was passed was
exhibited by tfie enthusiasts of the root
ing section. Indeed, a mix up occurred
which looked for a time as if it would end
in one of the prohibited rushes. It was
stopped by some cool-headed sophomores,
who realized the consequences of a
mix up.

STOCKTON, March 6.â The asylum au
thorities here place no credence in the
charges being made in San Francisco that
Merchant Rudde was railroaded into the
j madhouse. They say he was Insane,
i but that he must have had poor care in
! the way of nursing. Dr. Hersholt, who
had charge of Rudde, said to-day:
"The man was registered as 'Rudee'
here. He was insane. He continually
thought he was being persecuted. He was
also delirious. He had two large bed sores
when he came here. This was the fault
of the nurse in the hospital Rudee was
in at San Francisco. I a/lvised his rela
tives who visited him here that he was
â¢ going to die, and telephoned to them
I when he died on Friday. They came up
and got the body the following day, I
think the fight lies between the relatives
on Rudee's side and the relatives on the
side of his wife."

Aided Prisoners to Escape.
SANTA CRUZ, March 6.â Robert Alcorn
was given six months in the County Jail
this morning. He was from Bowlder
Creek and was charged with jail break
ing. One night he went to the City Prison
in that town, broke the lock off the door
and allowed some prisoners to escape. On
motion of the District Attorney, the
charge was reduced from felony to mis
demeanor.

MADE SICK BY
THE CANNED BEEF
Regulars Victims of the
Vile Food.
â  ___ â â â
TRIED THE STUFF IN STEWS
FINALLY THE WHOLE COMMAND
BROKE DOWN.
Lieutenant Munson of the Ninth In
fantry Tells How His Men.
Suffered for Lack of
Nourishment.
Special IMepatch to Tha Cail.
WASHINGTON, March 6.â The War
Department Court of Inquiry held a
very brief session to-day, the last be
fore its start for the West, where the
members will inspect the packing
plants of Chicago, Omaha and Kansas
City. There was but one witness ex
amined. Lieutenant Frederick L. Mun
son of the Ninth Infantry. He said that
during his service in Cuba, up to the
surrender of Santiago, his command
had as a meat component of the ration,
bacon and canned roast beef in the pro
portion of about ten days of the former
to fifteen days of the latter. Until the
surrender he received no complaints of
the canned roast beef, but after the
surrender hhs commissary sergeant re
ported to. him that something was mak
ing- the men very sick. They did not
know what it was, but thought it was
something they had eaten. After
eliminating every other fact that they
could think of, they concluded it must
be the canned roast beef.
He ordered the cooks to try fixing up
the meat in stews with hardtack and
tomatoes, but it seemed to make no dif
ference. The men continued sick, and
they then stopped the use of the beef.
On the voyage home they still had
canned roast beef, but the men did not
eat it. He tried to turn it In to the
commissary at Montauk, but the com
missary would not receive it.
"Did you make any report on this?"
asked Colonel Davis.
"No, sir."
"Did it not occur to you that it was
your duty to your command to report
this state of things?" continued Col
onel Davis, rather severely.
"As it appears to me now," said the
witness, very frankly, "I may have
been negligent, but at the time the com
plaint was so general, and there being
absolutely no chance of bettering the
situation, I did not make any formal
report on It."
As to the' refrigerator beef, witness
said the principal objection to it was
that it spoiled before the men received
it. He was obliged to condemn his
whole consignment of refrigerator beef
three times.
The health of the men remained good
until about two weeks before leaving,
when the command broke down all in
a bunch. His command was the head
quarters provost guard, made up of old
and picked men. They were not tne
sort to complain of anything until they
had reached the very limit of endur
ance. Their sickness was almost en
tirely bowel and stomach trouble.
This wound up the taking of testi
mony in Washington and the court ad
journed to meet in Chicago on Thurs
day. ______ââ_

apprehension that the bosses would cap- |
ture the primaries of the Non-Partisans,
elect delegates who would do tho bidding
ff the spoilsmen, and thus strike at the I
very vitals of the object of the Non-Par

didates.
After a discussion which extended over
more than two hours the chairman was
instructed to appoint a committee of live
to investigate the matter. The inmmiitee
is authorized o consult with attorneys
qualified to pass upon the merits of the
question Involved. When this committee
is ready t" report, the chairman <>f the
county committee will be notified, and he
will then call another meeting of the full
committee to take further action in the
matter.
Tt has been current rumor for some
time past that the Non-Partisans had in
contemplation a reorganization on a
broader basis than that which existed
during the last campaign, the proposed
new body to be known as the Municipal
League. ' In this connection it was re.
ported that the most prominent members
of the Merchants' Association would be
asked to co-operate with the league.
When Chairman Fitch was asked about
this he declined to discuss the matter,
saying that the first step necessary at
this time was to determine the effect of
the primary law. After this had b,i en
settled the problem of reorganization
would be considered. He added that the
Non-Partisans were opposed to the pri
mary law. and that If any grounds ex
isted for defeating it in the courts the
necessary legal action would be taken.

have been withheld by or misapplied by
them.
Attorney Monteith declares that the
case will be pressed energetically, and
that some interesting developments may
shortly be looked for.
