SOUGHT TO GAIN CONTROL
DEMANDED CHAIRMAN MANN'S
KESIGNATION.
Party Leaders Charge the Editor
With Having Broken His
Promise â A Thinly
Veiled Threat.
A deficiency of $4000 in the treasury nf
the Democratic State Central Committee,
a.< a result of its recent disastrous cam
paign, has brought to light a remark
able chapter in California Democratic
politics and also promises some coming
< ba pters of interest.
The story is nothing lessthan an attempt
on the part of W. R. Hearst to force Seth
Mann, chairman of the State Central
Committee, to resign, and to place M. F.
Tarpey, now and for a long time raan
ager of the Hearst estate, in the chair.
The story so far is known only to the
State executive committee and a few oth
ers in the confidential councils of the par
ty, but it will interest every member of
the party from Siskiyou to San Diego.
Mr. Hearst, it will be remembered, ar
ri\. il here from New York in the closing
days of the campaign. The party was
short of campaign funds about ten days
before election, and Seth Mann, who had
been named by Maguire as chairman of
the State Central Committee: W. P. Law
lor, chairman of the campaign committee,
and a few other party generals and staff
officers, faced the problem of how to
make a proper finish to the campaign
without money.
Jn this dilemma Messrs. Mann and Law
lÂ«.r called on Mr. Hearst ami stated the
and limitations of the campaign.
Mr. Hearst said in effect: â¢â¢â¢Jo right
ahead, gentlemen. Go to whatever ex
p< rise Is necessary and I will attend to
the bills."
This was definitely understood by the
i ampaign managers as a guarantee, that
bills Incurn â ! would be paid.
The following day the Examiner started
a subscription, agreeing to double all con
tributions. Tne day before i-lection the
contributions were announced as amount
nich Mr. Hearst increased
to $6018 60 for the benefit of the campaign.
When the campaign committee gut its
bills together it was found that tne f.\
pendlturea made und<-r Mr. Hearsts gu.ir
amounted to about $10,000, and that
there were bills outstanding to the ex
tent of about $-1000.
It was well along in December when the
Democratic managers sought to close up
the campaign accounts. Mr. Hearst had
gone Kast and MÂ«-ssrs. Mann and Lawlor
called on A. M. Lawrence, managing ed
itor of the Examiner, and inquired what
Mr. Hearst would be pleased to do about
the situation. The guarantee given by
Mr. Hearst was recalled and stated by the
two gentlemen.
Mr. Lawrence gave no satisfactory an
swer, but communicated with Mr. Hearst.
After a time Messrs. Mann and Lawlor
called again by appointment. Mr. Law
rence, as it is reliably stated, and as the
incident la told and understood by a num
ber of Democrats, informed his callers
that Mr. Hearst had no recollection of giv
ing a guarantee such as they had stated.
That was a disagreeable misunderstand
ing at best, but Mr. Lawrence had some
thing further to say. He informed Mr.
Mann and Mr. Lawlor that, regard!
the fact thai Mr. Hearst was not respon
sible for the deficit, Mr. Hearst would pay
the $4<fcÂ«Â» owed if Mr. Mann would resign
the chairmanship of th<- State Central
Committee and let Mr. Hearst name the
man to fill the vacancy.
This was simply an ufffr of $4000 for the
chairmanship ot the Democratic State
Central Committee of California. But Mr.
Lawrence went further and Intimated
: Mr. Mann did not resign it w,is
likely that the management of the cam
paign and of the current course of the
committee would be found open to criti
cism. It was further intimated that if
Mr. Mann would quietly get out of the
way he would receive a most flattering
notice in the Examiner for the ability he
had displayed, etc., and that resolutions
here and there to the Bame effect would
undoubtedly follow. Mr. Mann thus re
ceived a fairly decent but resolute bid
ding to get out.
Mr. Mann quietly Informed Mr. Law
rence that resigning was the last thing

It is said that not long after this a
prominent Democrat who had a little pat
ronage matter in hand went to Mr. Law
rence and asked him t<> Bee Chairman
Mann and get him to use his influence

"Oh. its no usp to sre Mann." said Mr.
Lawrence; ''Tarpey'll be chairman. He's
the man to see. '
Matters remained quiet for a time and
then in the Examiner of February 2 last
came a tap of the club as a gentle re
minder thai it w.-is about time for that
resignation, for that day the Examiner
jogged the committee and Mr. Mann in
the following editorial:
The Stratton primary election bill Is making
Its way rapidly toward, adoption. Has the
Democratic State Central Committee consid
ered the measure, as we suggest It should do?
We regret to say that it has not.
What is the committee for at a time like
this it not to grapple with problems affecting
the party welfare and to try to steer the party
clear of rocks? Is the committee so certain
that no constitutional law ran be framed as to
primaries that it feels no interest in the bills
before the Legislature which deals with party
elections? If it is not. how can it justify its
failure to FtiHy the Stratton bill and all other
bills of a kindred nature and to seek to influ
ence the Legislature to a wise course In dealing
with them? " V -
It seems to us that the committee should give
some proof that it Is not in a trance.

ocratic Central Club, the largest party
organization in the city, having a mem
bership of 1100, held a meeting at which

tracted no particular notice at the time.

aminer. In the light of explanations

the' passage of a primary law that shall stand
the tept of law and Democratic principles, and

sistance In our power in carrying out the same.
It. was Observed that the Examiner
avoided any mention of Mann or the com
pliment paid him in its report of the meet

its columns since the ultimatum was
given.
The campaign committee decided to
communicate with Mr. Hearst personally.

sent him and an evasive reply was re
ceived. Thereupon it was decided that the
I numeracy could survive without Mr
Hearst, and at a meeting of the executive

Mann was authorized to appoint a finance

Graves. L. P Byington, Oscar Hocks, F.
J. Fallon. Timothy Treacy. This com

Central Committe, which will probably be

Chairman Mann will not resign, Mr.
Tarpey will not be the chairman right
away and Mr. Hparst will nut be in con

THE END OF
ALL RELIGIOUS
CONTROVERSY
Bishop Montgomery
Declares It Near.
WEAPONS OF SCIENTISTS
MADE USEFUL IN UPHOLDING
THE CAUSE OF RELIGION.
Interesting and Instructive Lecture
Under the Auspices of Mission
Council, Young Men's
Institute.
The Right Rpv. George Montgom
ery. Bishop of Monterey and Los Angeles,
delivered a lecture in Metropolitan Temple
last night, which, while being Instructive
and entertaining, contained a powerful
argument against the unbeliever and so
called "scientist."
The lecture was provided by thf> T. M. I.
Central Bureau, and was given under the
auspices of Mission Council No. 3. The
hall was crowded from pit to gallery, and
hundreds of people were turned away long
before the opening of the exercises. Th<;
first number on the programme was an
organ voluntary by R. Fletcher Tilton.
D. J. O'Leary. president of Mission Coun
cil. ma.de a few opening remarks, and
John M. Burnett, president of the evening,
made the opening address. Solos were
rendered by Miss Millie Flynn and Charles
Parent Jr.
President Burnett then Introduced the
speaker of the evening. Bishop Montgom
ery, who announced as his subject "The
End of Controversy."
"The century just past," he said, has
been one of unusual activity in every
branch of business and study. With all
this energy in growth, expansion and
change of parties and politicians. In no
pursuit or business has activity been
greater than in religious questions, let
with all the false ideas and antagonisms
evolved, the church stands to-day the
most conspicuous object and organization.
Our work is largely one of controversy,
as it is our mission to overthrow false
doctrines and point out the true path. J
think the end of all this controversy must
soon come, and I expect it to come from
the very people who have caused the most
of itâ the educated and the cultured.
"Your unbeliever tells you that in things j
supernatural the only guide is reason. He j
says that God has put a brain into man to ;
reason things out for himself; that ho can
be independent and does not need a leader
or an authority. .
"Vet he will not apply this principle to
natural things. When it comes to a ques
tion of law, of medicine, aye. or even
smaller things as language, art and mu
sic does your scientist attempt to reason >
them out for himself, or will he not go
straightway and consult an authority on j
the subject? If it is a question of law he ;
goes to the books and re. iris in them what
has been evolved, not by any one man's
reasoning powers, but by a number of the |
best informed on that subject. And so it
Is with all things finite. Â»Â«*Â«.Â«
"When it comes to the infinite, to the
supernatural, however, he tells you that
his own reason is all sufficient: that he
needs consult no law. and that he was in
tended to construe the revelations of the
Bible to suit himself. Thus my friends,
brings about religious anarchy in just the
same manner as Bocial anarchy would be
brought about if each man were a lowed
to construe the social laws .â¢â¢.â¢cording to
his own reasoning power, and this keeps
us continually in a broil of controversy.
"The *-nd is near at hand. These scien
tists who have sought to overthrow relig
ion have only strengthened It. and the
wiser ones have come to realize it and are
daily looking upon religion with more
friendly ey! t In fact, it has come to such
a pass now that when yon find a man
orating about science, investigation will
show that be really knows little or noth
ing about true science.
COMMENDS THE PRESS
FOR FIGHTING BURNS
REV WOODWORTH'S TIMELY
APPROBATION.
Mini3ters Say Harsh Things About
Chief of Police Lees During an
Interesting Discussion.
Rev. John A. B. Wilson read an interesting
paper before the Presbyterian Ministerial
Union yesterday on "The Civic Duty of
the Pulpit." His clear and convincing
argument on why the pulpit should take
cognizance of important questions on local
government made a profound impression
on those â¢ present. The doctor spoke of
the evils rampant in the city and or the
small effort being made to control them.
He dwelt on the influence large cor
porations have shown in governmental af
fairs and on other important questions.
The paper provoked an animated discus
sion, prolonging the session half an hour
beyond the usual time.
Rev James Woodwortn alluding to the
criticism of the press contained in the
paper said:
While I am disgusted with the press at
times, yet I very often read something in it
that causes me to shout 'â bravo" and clap my
hands If it had not been for the press those
things that have taken place or been attempted
in Sacramento in connection with the Legis
lature during the past two months would have
been done and passed over with scarcely a
reference and D. M Burns would be the
Senator elect from this State to-day. Now,
that is fo, and I will defy anybody to deny it.
Rev Soo Hoo Nam Art of the Presby
terian Church and Mission addressed the
meeting in English, saying:
I have been preaching for several years
steadily here among my countrymen, but I am
sorry to say have accomplished hut little. In
some respects we have lost ground. Crime and
Vice are increasing in Chinatown and becom
ing more open and flagrant. A few years ago
Rambling In that quarter was restricted to the
alleys; now It flaunts openly in the most pub
\Vhen I Pay. "Why don't the police stoD
this?" I was told by my countrymen and
friends of whom I Inquired, "Why. don't you
know' They pay JHOO a week for police pro
tection and Immunity!" I can't prove these
things but I know their truth, and I am dis
couraged In the same way and for the same
reason the infamous traffic In slave girls la
increasing dally.
Dr. Wilson in closing thp discussion
paid the blame for the infamous condi
tion of things existing in Chinatown was
due to the attitude of the police. Yet,
said he, the trouble is not with the force
Itself, but with its head. The police fopce
can Btop this slave trade and close up
every gambling den. all of which are now
carried on openly, in one week when they
get orders from "upstairs" to do it. They
would do it ff they were told to. But,
as it is now, they dare not if they would.
The patrolmen are not to blame. You get
a patrolman to help you shut up one of
these gambling dens or to free a slave
girl and be will be "broken" inside of six
months and be relegated to a "goat-town"
beat at ov.ee
What better can you expect of a police
force, the head of -which is an habitue
of every prizefight that enmes off, and
who sits and"looks on with stoical indif
ference or a real interest while a man is
piunded out of semblance of a human be
ing?
